<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNconcurrence1.13" href="#FRconcurrence1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Put another way, even if a federal rule in most cases "really regulates procedure," <em>Sibbach</em>, 312 U.&nbsp;S., at 14, it does not "really regulat[e] procedure" when it displaces those rare state rules that, although "procedural" in the ordinary sense of the term, operate to define the rights and remedies available in a case.  This is so because what is procedural in one context may be substantive in another.  See <em>Hanna</em>, 380 U.&nbsp;S., at 471; <em>Guaranty Trust Co.</em> v. <em>York</em>, 326 U.&nbsp;S. 99, 108 (1945).   </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNconcurrence1.14" href="#FRconcurrence1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Although this Court's decision in <em>Hanna </em>cited <em>Sibbach</em>, that is of little significance.  <em>Hanna </em>did not hold that any seemingly procedural federal rule will always govern, even when it alters a substantive state right; nor, as in <em>Sibbach</em>, was the argument that I now make before the Court.  Indeed, in <em>Hanna</em> we cited <em>Sibbach</em>'s statement that the Enabling Act prohibits federal rules that alter the rights to be adjudicated by the litigants, 312 U.&nbsp;S., at 13-14, for the proposition that "a court, in measuring a Federal Rule against the standards contained in the Enabling Act ... need not wholly blind itself to the degree to which the Rule makes the character and result of the federal litigation stray from the course it would follow in state courts,"  380 U.&nbsp;S., at 473.  And most of our subsequent decisions that have squarely addressed the framework for applying federal rules in diversity cases have not mentioned <em>Sibbach </em>at all but cited only <em>Hanna.</em>  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Burlington Northern R. Co.</em> v. <em>Woods</em>, 480 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 5 (1987).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> notes that in <em>Mississippi Publishing Corp.</em> v. <em>Murphree</em>, 326 U.&nbsp;S. 438 (1946), we used language that supported his view.  See <em>ante</em>, at 13.  But in that case, we contemplated only that the Federal Rule in question might have "incidental effects ... upon the rights of litigants," explaining that "[t]he fact that the application of Rule 4(f) will operate to subject petitioner's rights to adjudication by the district court for northern Mississippi" rather than southern Mississippi "will undoubtedly affect those rights."  326 U.&nbsp;S., at 445-446.  There was no suggestion that by affecting the method of enforcing the rights in that case, the federal rules could plausibly abridge, enlarge, or modify the rights themselves.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNconcurrence1.15" href="#FRconcurrence1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor do I see how it follows from the dissent's premises that a class cannot be certified.  The dissent contends that 901(b) is a damages "limitation," <em>post</em>, at 7, n.&nbsp;2, 8, 9, 12, 25, or "proscription," <em>post</em>, at 12, n.&nbsp;6, 21, whereas Rule 23 "does not command that a particular remedy be available when a party sues in a representative capacity," <em>post</em>, at 11, and that consequently both provisions can apply.  Yet even if the dissent's premises were correct, Rule 23 would still control the question whether petitioner may certify a class, and 901(b) would be relevant only to determine whether petitioner, at the conclusion of a class-action lawsuit, may collect statutory damages.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It may be that if the dissent's interpretation of 901(b) were correct, this class could not (or has not) alleged sufficient damages for the federal court to have jurisdiction, see 28 U. S.&nbsp;C. 1332(d)(6).  But that issue was not raised in respondent's motion to dismiss (from which the case comes to this Court), and it was not squarely presented to the Court.  In any event, although the lead plaintiff has "acknowledged that its individual claim" is for less than the required amount in controversy, see 549 F.&nbsp;3d 137, 140 (CA2 2008), we do not know what actual damages the entire class can allege.  Thus, even if the Court were to adopt all of the dissent's premises, I believe the correct disposition would be to vacate and remand for further consideration of whether the required amount in controversy has or can be met.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNconcurrence1.16" href="#FRconcurrence1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In its <em>Erie </em>analysis, the dissent observes that when sovereigns create laws, the enacting legislatures sometimes assume those laws will apply only within their territory.  See <em>post</em>, at 18-20.  That is a true fact, but it does not do very much work for the dissent's position.  For one thing, as the dissent observes, this <em>Erie </em>analysis is relevant only if there is no conflict between Rule 23 and 901(b), and the court can thus apply both.  <em>Post</em>, at 17.  But because, in my view, Rule 23 applies, the only question is whether it would violate the Enabling Act.  See <em>Hanna</em>, 380 U.&nbsp;S., at 471.   And that inquiry is different from the Rules of Decision Act, or <em>Erie</em>, inquiry.  See <em>id.</em>, at 469-471.</p>
<p>

The dissent's citations, moreover, highlight simply that when interpreting statutes, context matters.  Thus, we sometimes presume that laws cover only domestic conduct and sometimes do not, depending upon, <em>inter alia</em>, whether it makes sense in a given situation to assume that "the character of an act as lawful or unlawful must be determined wholly by the law of the [place] where the act is done," <em>American Banana Co.</em> v. <em>United Fruit Co.</em>, 213 U.&nbsp;S. 347, 356 (1909).  But in the context of 901(b), a presumption against extraterritoriality makes little sense.  That presumption applies almost only to laws governing what people can or cannot do.  Section 901(b), however, is not directed to the conduct of persons but is instead directed to New York courts.  Thus, 901(b) is, by its own terms, not extraterritorial insofar as it states that it governs <em>New York</em> courts.  It is possible that the New York Legislature simply did not realize that New York courts hear claims under other sources of law and that other courts hear claims under New York law, and therefore mistakenly believed that they had written a limit on New York remedies.  But because New York set up 901(b) as a general rule about how its courts operate, my strong presumption is to the contrary.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNconcurrence1.17" href="#FRconcurrence1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To be sure, one could imagine the converse story, that a legislature would create statutory penalties but dictate that such penalties apply only when necessary to overcome the costs and inconvenience of filing a lawsuit, and thus are not necessary in a class action.  But it is hard to see how that narrative applies to New York, given that New York's penalty provisions, on their face, apply to all plaintiffs, be they class or individual, and that 901(b) addresses penalties that are created under any source of state or federal law.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNconcurrence1.18" href="#FRconcurrence1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg </small></span>asserts that class certification in this matter would "transform a $500 case into a $5,000,000 award."  <em>Post</em>, at 1.  But in fact, class certification would transform 10,000 $500 cases into one $5,000,000 case.  It may be that without class certification, not all of the potential plaintiffs would bring their cases.  But that is true of any procedural vehicle; without a lower filing fee, a conveniently located courthouse, easy-to-use federal procedural rules, or many other features of the federal courts, many plaintiffs would not sue.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent1.1" href="#FRdissent1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Rules of Decision Act directs that, "[t]he laws of the several states, except where the Constitution or treaties of the United States or Acts of Congress otherwise require or provide, shall be regarded as rules of decision in civil actions in the courts of the United States, in cases where they apply."  28 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1652.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent1.2" href="#FRdissent1.2">2</a></strong><br><p><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Stevens</small></span> stakes out common ground on this point:  "[F]ederal rules," he observes, "must be interpreted with some degree of 'sensitivity to important state interests and regulatory policies,' ... and applied to diversity cases against the background of Congress' command that such rules not alter substantive rights and with consideration of 'the degree to which the Rule makes the character and result of the federal litigation stray from the course it would follow in state courts,' <em>Hanna</em> [v. <em>Plumer</em>], 380 U.&nbsp;S. [460, 473 (1965)]."  <em>Ante</em>, at 3. (opinion concurring in part and concurring in judgment).  See also <em>ante</em>, at 4 ("A 'state procedural rule, though undeniably procedural in the ordinary sense of the term' may exist 'to influence substantive outcomes,' ... and may in some instances become so bound up with the state-created right or remedy that it defines the scope of that substantive right or remedy." (some internal quotation marks omitted)); <em>ante</em>, at 5 ("When a State chooses to use a traditionally procedural vehicle as a means of defining the scope of substantive rights or remedies, federal courts must recognize and respect that choice.").  Nevertheless, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Stevens </small></span>sees no reason to read Rule 23 with restraint in this particular case; the Federal Rule preempts New York's damages limitation, in his view, because 901(b) is "a procedural rule that is not part of New York's substantive law."  <em>Ante</em>, at 1.  This characterization of 901(b) does not mirror reality, as I later explain.  See <em>infra</em>, at 17-24.  But a majority of this Court, it bears emphasis, agrees that Federal Rules should be read with moderation in diversity suits to accommodate important state concerns.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent1.3" href="#FRdissent1.3">3</a></strong><br><p> Even in the mine-run case, a class action can result in "potentially ruinous liability."  Advisory Committee's Notes on Fed. Rule Civ. Proc.&nbsp;23, 28 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. App., p. 143.  A court's decision to certify a class accordingly places pressure on the defendant to settle even unmeritorious claims.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Coopers &amp; Lybrand</em> v. <em>Livesay</em>, 437 U.&nbsp;S. 463, 476 (1978).  When representative plaintiffs seek statutory damages, pressure to settle may be heightened because a class action poses the risk of massive liability unmoored to actual injury.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Ratner</em> v. <em>Chemical Bank New York Trust Co.</em>, 54 F.&nbsp;R.&nbsp;D. 412, 416 (SDNY 1972) (exercising "considerable discretion of a pragmatic nature" to refuse to certify a class because the plaintiffs suffered negligible actual damages but sought statutory damages of $13,000,000).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNdissent1.4" href="#FRdissent1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>In re Automotive Refinishing Paint Antitrust Litigation</em>, 515 F. Supp. 2d 544, 549-551 (ED Pa. 2007); <em>Leider</em> v. <em>Ralfe</em>, 387 F. Supp. 2d 283, 289-292 (SDNY 2005); <em>Dornberger </em>v. <em>Metropolitan Life Insurance Co</em>., 182 F. R. D. 72, 84 (SDNY 1999).  See also <em>Weber</em> v. <em>U.&nbsp;S. Sterling Securities, Inc.</em>, 282 Conn. 722, 738-739, 914 A.&nbsp;2d 816, 827-828 (2007) (section 901(b) applied in Connecticut state court to action governed by New York substantive law).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNdissent1.5" href="#FRdissent1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Shady Grove projects that a dispensation in favor of Allstate would require "courts in all diversity class actions . . . [to] look to state rules and decisional law rather than to Rule 23 . . . in making their class certification decisions."  Brief for Petitioner 55.  This slippery-slope projection is both familiar and false.  Cf. R. Bork, The Tempting of America 169 (1990) ("Judges and lawyers live on the slippery slope of analogies; they are not supposed to ski it to the bottom.").  In this case, CPLR 901(a) lists the state-law prerequisites for class certification, but Allstate does not contend that 901(a) overrides Rule 23.  Brief for Respondent 18 ("There is no dispute that the criteria for class certification under state law do not apply in federal court; that is the ground squarely occupied by Rule 23.").  Federal courts sitting in diversity have routinely applied Rule 23's certification standards, rather than comparable state provisions.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>In&nbsp;re New Motor Vehicles Canadian Export Antitrust Litigation</em>, 522 F.&nbsp;3d 6, 18-24 (CA1 2008); Order and Reasons in <em>In&nbsp;re Katrina Canal Breaches Consolidated Litigation</em>, Civ. Action No. 05-4182 (ED La., Aug. 6, 2009).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNdissent1.6" href="#FRdissent1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court disputes the strength of the evidence of legislative intent, see <em>ante</em>, at 9, but offers no alternative account of 901(b)'s purpose.  Perhaps this silence indicates how very hard it would be to ascribe to 901(b) <em>any</em> purpose bound up with the fairness and efficiency of processing cases.  On its face, the proscription is concerned with remedies, <em>i.e.</em>, the availability of statutory damages in a lawsuit.  Legislative history confirms this objective, but is not essential to revealing it.  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNdissent1.7" href="#FRdissent1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is, of course, a difference between "justly administering [a] remedy," <em>Sibbach</em> v. <em>Wilson &amp; Co.</em>, 312 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 14 (1941), and prescribing the content of that remedy; if Rule 23 can be read to increase a plaintiff's recovery from $1,000,000 to some greater amount, the Rule has arguably "enlarge[d] . . . [a] substantive right" in violation of the Rules Enabling Act.  28 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 2072(b).  The plurality appears to acknowledge this point, stating that the Federal Rules we have found to be in compliance with the Act have not "altered . . . available remedies."  <em>Ante</em>, at 13.  But the Court's relentless reading of Rule 23 today does exactly that: The Federal Rule, it says, authorizes the recovery of class-size statutory damages even though the New York provision instructs that such penalties shall not be available.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNdissent1.8" href="#FRdissent1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court states that "[w]e cannot rewrite [a state law] to reflect our perception of legislative purpose."  <em>Ante</em>, at 9.  But we can, of course, <em>interpret the</em> <em>Federal Rules</em> in light of a State's regulatory policy to decide whether and to what extent a Rule preempts state law.  See <em>supra</em>, at 3-7.  Just as we read Federal Rule 3 in <em>Walker </em>v.<em> Armco Steel Corp., </em>446 U.&nbsp;S. 740, 751 (1980), not to govern when a suit commences for purposes of tolling a state statute of limitations (although the Rule indisputably controls when an action commences for federal procedural purposes), so too we could read Rule 23 not to direct when a class action may be maintained for purposes of recovering statutory damages prescribed by state law.  On this reading of Rule 23, no rewriting of 901(b) is necessary to avoid a conflict.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNdissent1.9" href="#FRdissent1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The New York Legislature appears to have anticipated this result.  In discussing the remedial bar effected by 901(b), the bill's sponsor explained that a<em> </em>"statutory class action for actual damages would still be permissible."  S. Fink, [Sponsor's] Memorandum, Bill Jacket, L. 1975, Ch. 207.  See also State Consumer Protection Board Memorandum (May 29, 1975), Bill Jacket, L. 1975, Ch. 207.  On this understanding, New York courts routinely authorize class actions when the class waives its right to receive statutory penalties.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Cox</em> v. <em>Microsoft Corp.</em>, 8 App. Div. 3d 39, 778 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;S. 2d 147 (2004); <em>Pesantez</em> v. <em>Boyle Env. Servs., Inc.</em>, 251 App. Div. 2d 11, 673 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;S. 2d 659 (1998); <em>Ridge Meadows Homeowners' Assn., Inc.</em> v. <em>Tara Development Co.</em>, 242 App.&nbsp;Div. 2d 947, 665 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;S. 2d 361 (1997); <em>Super Glue Corp.</em> v. <em>Avis Rent A Car System, Inc.</em>, 132 App. Div. 2d 604, 517 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;S. 2d 764 (1987); <em>Weinberg</em> v. <em>Hertz Corp.</em>, 116 App.&nbsp;Div. 2d 1, 499 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;S. 2d 693 (1986).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNdissent1.10" href="#FRdissent1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality notes that "we have rejected every statutory challenge to a Federal Rule that has come before us."  <em>Ante</em>, at 13.  But it omits that we have interpreted Rules with due restraint, including Rule 23, thus diminishing prospects for the success of such challenges.  See <em>Ortiz</em> v. <em>Fibreboard Corp.</em>, 527 U.&nbsp;S. 815, 842 (1999); <em>Amchem Products, Inc.</em> v. <em>Windsor</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 591, 612-613 (1997); <em>supra</em>, at 4-8.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNdissent1.11" href="#FRdissent1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;States have adopted a variety of formulations to limit the use of class actions to gain certain remedies or to pursue certain claims, as illustrated by the 96 examples listed in Allstate's brief.  Apps. to Brief for Respondent.  The Court's "one-size-fits-all" reading of Rule 23, <em>ante</em>, at 4, likely prevents the enforcement of <em>all</em> of these statutes in diversity actions--including the numerous state statutory provisions that, like 901(b), attempt to curb the recovery of statutory damages.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> Cal. Civ. Code Ann. 2988.5(a)(2) (West 1993); Colo. Rev. Stat. Ann. 12-14.5-235(d) (2009); Conn. Gen. Stat. 36a-683(a) (2009); Haw. Rev. Stat. 489-7.5(b)(1) (2008); Ind. Code 24-4.5-5-203(a)(2) (2004); Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. 367.983(1)(c) (West 2006); Mass. Gen. Laws, ch. 167B, 20(a)(2)(B) (2008); Mich. Comp. Laws Ann. 493.112(3)(c) (West 2005); N.&nbsp;M. Stat. Ann. 58-16-15(B) (Lexis 2004); Ohio Rev. Code Ann. 1351.08(A) (West 2004); Okla. Stat., Tit. 14A, 5-203(1) (2007 Supp.); Wyo. Stat. Ann. 40-19-119(a)(iii) (2009).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNdissent1.12" href="#FRdissent1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Shady Grove's argument that 901(b) is procedural based on its possible application to foreign claims is also out of sync with our <em>Erie </em>decisions, many of them involving state statutes of similarly unqualified scope.  The New Jersey law at issue in <em>Cohen</em> v. <em>Beneficial Industrial Loan Corp.</em>, 337 U.&nbsp;S. 541, 544, n.&nbsp;1 (1949), for example, required plaintiffs to post a bond as security for costs in "<em>any</em> [stockholder's derivative] action."  (quoting 1945 N.&nbsp;J. Laws ch. 131 (emphasis added)).  See also, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Walker</em>, 446 U.&nbsp;S., at 742-743, and n.&nbsp;4 (Oklahoma statute deemed "[a]n action" commenced for purposes of the statute of limitations upon service of the summons (quoting Okla. Stat., Tit. 12, 97 (1971)).  Our characterization of a state statute as substantive for <em>Erie</em> purposes has never hinged on whether the law applied only to domestic causes of action.  To the contrary, we have ranked as substantive a variety of state laws that the state courts apply to federal and out-of-state claims, including statutes of limitations and burden-of-proof prescriptions.  See <em>infra</em>, at 21.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNdissent1.13" href="#FRdissent1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In contrast, many "state rules ostensibly addressed to procedure," <em>ante</em>, at 10 (majority opinion)--including pleading standards and rules governing summary judgment, pretrial discovery, and the admissibility of certain evidence--would not so hugely impact forum choices.  It is difficult to imagine a scenario that would promote more forum shopping than one in which the difference between filing in state and federal court is the difference between a potential award of $500 and one of $5,000,000.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNdissent1.14" href="#FRdissent1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is no question that federal courts can "give effect to the substantive thrust of [901(b)] without untoward alteration of the federal scheme for the trial and decision of civil cases."  <em>Gasperini</em>, 518 U. S., at 426.  There is no risk that individual plaintiffs seeking statutory penalties will flood federal courts with state-law claims that could be managed more efficiently on a class basis; the diversity statute's amount-in-controversy requirement ensures that small state-law disputes remain in state court.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNdissent1.15" href="#FRdissent1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It remains open to Congress, of course, to exclude from federal-court jurisdiction under the Class Action Fairness Act of 2005, 28 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1332(d), claims that could not be maintained as a class action in state court.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

    <div class="lawyer-search">
    <h2 class="lawyer-search__search-title">Need to find an attorney?</h2>
    <p class="lawyer-search__search-subtitle">Search our directory by legal issue</p>
    <p class="lawyer-search__form-instructions">Enter information in one or both fields (Required)</p>
    <form class="lawyer-search__form" method="get" action="https://lawyers.findlaw.com/lawyer/lawyer_dir/search/jsp/stdSearch_process.jsp">
        <div class="lawyer-search__input-group lawyer-search__input-group--legal-issue">
            <label class="lawyer-search__input-label" for="lawyer-search__input--legal-issue-1">Legal issue</label>
            <input class="lawyer-search__input" name="keyword" type="search" data-title=".search-title" placeholder="" maxlength="255" autocomplete="off" aria-autocomplete="both" aria-haspopup="listbox" aria-expanded="false" role="combobox" id="lawyer-search__input--legal-issue-1" data-target="lawyer-search__autocomplete--legal-issue-1" aria-controls="lawyer-search__autocomplete--legal-issue-1">
            <button class="lawyer-search__input-clear" type="button" aria-label="Clear input">
                <i class="fa fa-times-circle" aria-hidden="true"></i>
            </button>
            <div class="lawyer-search__autocomplete legal-issue-input-dropdown" id="lawyer-search__autocomplete--legal-issue-1">
                <ul class="legal-issue-input-dropdown__list legal-issue-input-dropdown__list--results" role="listbox"></ul>
                <div class="legal-issue-input-dropdown__separator">More Options</div>

    <script src="//caselaw.findlaw.com/static/fi/images/etc/designs/flcommon/flfe/javascript/lp.redesign.js"></script><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {

        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);

          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);

      });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
